I know this is waaay old to be commenting on, but I think a mistake that you didn't cover was specifically the Cell Processor architecture that Sony chose for the PS3. They used an odd offshoot of the PowerPC architecture called the Cell Processor. It was supposed to be a revolutionary venture between Sony and IBM, but the chip was difficult to work with especially for early 00's tech. Games generally suffered because of these difficulties, either with production delays to work out programming to deal with quirks with how the chip handled data or bugs that would eventually require being Day 1 patched via PSN. While the Cell Processor was indeed useful when used with various Unix/Linux, the US DOD famously had bought several PS3s and turned them into a computing cluster which was negatively affected by the '10 decision regarding Other OS being patched out. The PS3 as a whole set back Sony quite a bit from my reconing.

Going against the grain with their hardware cost Sony the generation as much as their dumb launch did.

While I do think those were big mistakes by Sony, in my opinion the *_BIGGEST_* mistake Sony has made was not upholding with their 'Backwards Compatibility' model that they initially started and succesfully implemented back with the PSOne, PS2 and even early PS3. This has led to multiple issues since the PS3 with not only hardware and software compatibility but, also things like game preservation concerns and Sony's apparent apathy regarding these issues.  It's obvious that they are so out of touch with their customer base that only when the customer base began to complain amidst reports that the PSN store would begin shutting down branches dedicated to PS3 and Vita, they then were jolted into seeing how there is a huge part of their customer base that still enjoys playing the older titles from their previous gen hardware.   Not to mention, having one of their "higher up's" basically question why anybody would want to play older games from the PSOne, PS2 & PS3 libraries did not help endear them to their customer base. If they had kept up with their backwards compatibility model, I think they would've dominated the market in so many ways. Especially, if they found a way to also include most, if not all, titles from their PSP and PSVita libraries in that model. Vita memory cards were a paradox. It staved off piracy for years, and the system retained a high software attachment rate, which is why the console got so much software support and limited physical releases for all of it's lifespan despite the system's lack of sales. The system was eventually broken wide open regardless of firmware and exploit title availability, but they staved it off for a good 5 years. This meant it saw continuing software sales and digital / physical support at a higher rate, for longer than the PSP did... since the PSP was completely broke wide open.  The system was ultimately the same price as the 3DS except with far better graphics, much better screen, build and more features. However, they could have just bundled the system with 32GB built in memory, sold cards for expansion, charged $100 more since it was a much more premium handheld, or kept it the same price, taken a big loss on hardware, then tried to make it up in software sales and optional peripherals.  However, people always ignored the fact that physical game cards didn't require a memory card, barring a few rare exceptions. The most price conscious consumers tended to be physical hold outs who still went to Gamestop expecting to find dirt cheap used copies... so, theoretically, most of those unwilling to pay for memory would have been completely disinterested in buying digital games anyway.  Additionally, the initial plan was to sell digital games $5 cheaper than MSRP for physical titles, which would've meant that those who bought a memory card, and bought enough games to warrant needing 32-64GB of memory, would have recouped costs over time through savings on digital buys. Convoluted and rarely explained by Sony or any media outlets, but understandable. It would have been analogous to paying thousands of dollars for an amazing gaming PC, but then saving thousands on ultra cheap digital game sales on places like Steam or Humble etc.  The alternative could have been allowing SD cards, but then taking continuous, aggressive legal action against perpetrators like Nintendo does. PSP was destroyed by piracy and the software sales attach rate became non-existent as piracy became so easy and so pervasive... there's always this idea that people will only do it if they have a vendetta against anti-piracy measures, or  that it will still be too out-of-the-way for most consumers to bother. I don't think the PSP bears this out, as by the end of it's generation, the only reason people were buying PSP's was to turn them into emulation machines.

I don't follow the industry closely, but I recall hearing of some PSx hardware using "cell architecture", making it difficult for third-party developers to create games for it, especially those attempting cross-system titles.  The developers weren't familiar with the hardware (or didn't get adequate support), resulting in the Xbox versions always looking better.  Given that the PSx cost more than an XBox, gamers had less incentive to go with that platform.

The PS3 launch seemed like a disaster at the time, but Sony were playing the long game.  They knew that the PS2 had almost singlehandedly made DVD as a format and ended the reign of VHS.  Sony wanted to do the same with PS3 and Blu-ray, and IT WORKED PERFECTLY.  The fact that the Xbox One came with an internal Blu-ray drive says it all.  By the end of that generation, despite the slow and late start, the PS3 had outsold the Xbox 360, AND Sony were getting a nice juicy cut of every movie release and Blu-ray player sale.  The Xbox team were so awed by the wisdom of this approach, they tried to replicate it with the launch of the Xbox One.  Microsoft successfully predicted the rise of digital game distribution and always-on connectivity, but implemented it in a way that reduced value for gamers, rather than delivering an superior product for the premium price.  And this isn't coming from some Playstation fanboy - I was Team Xbox during both those generations, primarily due to Forza.

This is an old video so I doubt this comment'll be seen, but one of Sony's worst non-gaming mistakes was using faulty clutches from a camera model in the ERS-210 model Aibo, which released in 2000. However, Sony knew the clutches were faulty when they chose to use them when manufacturing the Aibo.   Because of the faulty hardware, every non-"supercore" (what they called the hardware revision that was released sometime after the initial model) ERS-210 Aibo experiences a complete failure due to faulty clutches in the neck of the robot. Some failed within a year of purchase, while most failed after the 1 year warranty expired.   Over 50,000 original model ERS-210s with faulty hardware were sold (unknown figures on how many of the revised model were sold) at ~$1,500 each -- over $75 million. Since the robots typically broke after the warranty ended, many people had to pay out of pocket to have their Aibo repaired, all due to a known issue with the clutches they used. And because of the proprietary nature of basically everything inside of the Aibos (thanks, Sony!), it was thought impossible for years for the robots to be fixable outside of Sony's repair centers.   (nearly every model of aibo also uses the sony memory sticks)

I'd say you nailed the 1st and 3rd mistakes, spot on.  The 2nd one though I'd say in hindsight, is absolutely wrong.  I'd call the launch of the PS3 a complete success, as the inclusion of the Blu-Ray drive ended up being an excellent decision for them in the long term.    Sure, it hurt them somewhat starting out, but a console's lifespan consists of many years.  It's a marathon, not a sprint.  Just as the PS2 helped make DVD successful, so did the PS3 for Blu-Ray.  If you don't call the PS2 launch a mistake then I don't see how you can call the PS3 one either.
You have to remember though that this was Sony's very first time offering an online experience with a home console, and it got much, MUCH better over time.  Naturally it wasn't going to be that great at launch.  Can't really judge it for that.
Hell, Sony did the EXACT same thing with the PS2, as DVD was fairly new at the time as well!  And it ended up being a brilliant move.  DVD went on to become the dominant format.  And Blu-Ray also very much became the next gen format as well.  Blu-Ray would not be what it is today without the launch of the PS3.  I'd say it was anything but a mistake.
Hell, those in the tech world knew how big Blu-Ray was going to be, and we all knew that the $600 price tag was actually quite a good deal at the time.  You were definitely getting your moneys worth with a PS3 at the time, even if some of the less knowledgeable public didn't know this.  If you chalk this up to Sony not knowing their consumer base, what would you have them do, use DVD's in the PS3?    This of course would have given us a much cheaper system, but Blu-Ray would not be the success it is today without the launch of the PS3.  I would say in the end this ended up being a BIG win for them, so I really can't see this as a mistake as it most definitely was not.  Most of these mistakes are seen as so based on hindsight, and hindsight shows that it was anything but a mistake.
I'd say the proprietary memory cards may have been one of THE biggest mistakes for the PS Vita, and was probably a BIG reason why it failed.  I can only imagine how many more digital downloads they would have sold had they either used MicroSD cards or had their own memory cards been priced to compete with MicroSD cards.  What a HUGE misstep from Sony that was!
Oh god yes... Those damned proprietary memory types!  God I hated those!  Hope if Sony ever gets back into the portable market that they learn from this mistake!

Adding onto the cost of the PS3 (and Sony's hubris) was Sony's proprietary cell architecture. It was a nightmare to develop for. Gabe Newell called the PS3 a "waste of everybody's" time due to how crappy the architecture was. Sony's thinking was the radically different, complex workings of the PS3 would force developers to solely develop for the PS3, leaving little time for other systems. In reality many multiplatform 7th-gen games looked and ran worse on PS3 than the 360, especially early on before developers figured out the cell architecture.

First of all, you make some fantastic content. I started watching you when the Tetris documentary came out...and it became the first hour-long youtube video I ever watched. I loved all of your other videos...and this one brought up some good points. But...there are a few things you said that I think miss the mark.  So when you went in to the part about Sony and their proprietary formats; I felt it was very unfair to lump Betamax, DAT, Minidisc...and even Memory Stick in that list.   I'll start with Betamax. I know the old tale is "it died because of Sony"...and that might be part of the reason. But it's far from what I would call a proprietary format. Yes...it had licensing requirements...they were much stricter than VHS. But...that is the same for any media format that's come out in history. Betamax was just one of two main standards for video storage aimed at the consumer market...and it was also the first to hit the market. I can't speak for what the situation was like in the 70s....Sony may have kept the format all to itself. But that did change when JVC came out with VHS and made it a more open-standard. There were other brands of Betamax units on the market; I personally have two Toshibas and a Sanyo top-loader....I have exactly 0 Sony Betamax units in my collection. I also have blank media from brands like Memorex, Maxell, TDK, Scotch....all of the major players. VHS may have made Sony back-off a little on the licensing; the two were in fierce competition in the 80's. Houses were either Beta or VHS. This was also an era where having a VCR was...rare. The reality is by the time VCRs became ubiquitous....Beta was largely done for. It lost the war....but I would not say it lost it because of being proprietary. I could record to a Scotch branded Betamax blank tape in my Toshiba Beta VCR...and play it back in any other Beta VCR. It was just one standard that didn't make it. Whether or not Sony making it open format from the start would have saved it...we'll never know.   While I don't know much about the DAT market....it was a high-end niche format. Storing digital audio on tape had been done since the 70s in the professional arena; Sony was one of the few leading this by selling PCM adaptors for video machines...most notably UMatic. In fact just about *every* system for doing digital audio on to tape was proprietary. But to call DAT proprietary is still incorrect;  it was not a Sony only product...even if you had to pay royalties to Sony. Several other manufacturers made DAT decks....all adhering to the DAT standard. Let's not forget that the compact disc...which we all know how successful it was; could have also been called a "proprietary" format at the start...and it was controlled by Sony and Philips. The difference was as the format "took hold"....more and more companies started to license it.   Minidisc also wasn't proprietary for the same reasons. Companies like Sharp and Aiwa also made and sold Minidisc equipment. Yes...it was Sony's format all the way around, even down to the audio codec used on the disc. Blank media was available from several companies...not just Sony. It only "feels" proprietary because the format was a flop in the consumer market.   You didn't even mention Digital Compact Cassette; which was the competition to Minidisc; but flopped harder than DAT or MD.  One could argue in the 70's that cassette tapes were proprietary because they didn't play in your 8-track player; or that 8-tracks were proprietary because you couldn't play them on your phonograph. They were just....standards. Some made it, some failed; but I would hesitate to call Beta, MD, or DAT a "proprietary" format. They were just niche and for the most part, simply rejected by the consumer market.  MemoryStick was just the result of the storage card wars; Sony created memory stick...they didn't have to pay any patents or royalties to use it. It was annoying when it lost...but they had hopes it would take over in a world where CompactFlash had domainated because it was "it".
SNK‚Äôs 5 Biggest mistakes

While old and well-traveled, I still think the BetaMax was a top-3.  While we think about VHS as an older version of DVD/Blu-Ray, that was simply NOT the case.  The primary utility of VHS/Beta was NOT to watch movies.  Home theater wasn't a thing and movie rentals wouldn't be a thing for several years afterwards.  The primary utility at launch was "time-shifting" and recording function.  You could go out to eat and not miss Dallas.  You didn't have to stay up to midnight to watch SNL.  Remember, there were only a few channels at the time, no streaming video, no way to immediately see a show you missed, etc.  This was huge at the time. And, Sony screwed this up (the implementation of the technology) in some key ways that disadvantaged the technology.  I don't know the value of the consumer video tape market in the 1980s and 1990s, but I bet it was much more significant than the PS3 launch issues.
Sony's decision about the memory sticks, especially in the case of the Pro Duo, wasn't unreasonable. Whether it would turn out to be the right call depended on the execution. That is where the PSP and PSVita's cases are completely different. Back in the 2005-2011 era, the Pro Duo memory sticks were light years ahead of regular SD cards in terms of speed (which is especially important for a disc based console) and despite being on the expensive side they were still relatively affordable and also very well-built. In the Vita's case, Sony messed up big time. While the Vita cards were still a lot faster than regular Micro-SD cards, they were insultingly overpriced and extremely poorly made. These cards would just get fried on their own after a couple of years of use and according to many users, that issue became worse the higher the capacity of the card was. So the more money you paid, the worse the already overpriced product you got was. Sony could have honestly done something with their portable memory solution which focused primarily on speed and not so much capacity. The Pro Duo was a good first step but they totally gave up/half-assed things after that. Not that we have anything to complain about regarding that these days though.

Regarding the PSN hack: Yes, Sony definitely bungled the handling of the issue. But personally I find it interesting how cause and effect keep getting mixed up when people report on the issue ten years after the fact. Yes, Sony basically kicked off the issue by removing the OtherOS feature, then a public jailbreak was revealed, then Sony started lawsuits, then the hacks happened. In hindsight, this was a bad move on Sony's part. However, what people keep forgetting is that Sony removed the OtherOS feature AFTER hackers had announced working on a vulnerability that might allow to jailbreak the system through OtherOS. True, it wasn't a full jailbreak (yet), but it was in reaction to this development that Sony removed OtherOS support. I was working for an IT news outlet back in the day, and everyone there felt that this was just a natural reaction on Sony's side - of course the would remove OtherOS support as soon as people would start using it to hack the system! Somehow, this little nugget of information keeps getting omitted when looking back on the events, giving the impression that Sony just removed OtherOS for no reason at all.

The PS3 itself was also a horrible system.  It was VERY badly designed, on purpose, to make it hard to develop for.  They gambled that people would develop games for it, and then not port it to other consoles because the PS3 took so many resources.  It backfired, instead games were made for everything but the PS3.  They designed the system with the expectation that the Cell was so powerful that it wouldn't need a GPU.  LOL.  Hint: it wasn't, it was orders of magnitude less powerful than what they claimed.  It actually wasn't very powerful at all, so they had to add a GPU at the last minute, cutting the RAM of the system in half to make up some of the cost (so it wasn't $800).  The Xbox 360 was also garbage, but that was just in build quality, not so much in design/performance.  It had the largest failure rate ever of any console.  The PS5 also shows some early signs of being designed more like the PS3 than the PS4...much more expensive, and basically lying out their asses to hype it.

So... the bread and butter here.  1) PS3 launching for $599. As SONY trounced the competition in the Playstation 2 era... all they had to do was not massively screw it up. And, at least for the first half of the generation, they did. So many oversights, including not settling the lawsuit and licensing the rumble technology as well as forcing blu-ray just a little bit too early. I think they could have gone DVD-9 (dual layer DVD's) and had plenty of space at 9GB. The plan would have been for the console to live roughly 4 years as they got a year behind the 360. I'd have planned for the PS4 to launch 4 years after the PS3 did. Would have allowed the PS3 to be much much cheaper, come out sooner, and a smoother transition into the PS4 without a herd of people still a bit sore from paying nearly $650 after tax for just the console....many paying MUCH more, scalpers were in hog heaven. Supply was so low, because yield on making the bluray lasers was so poor. IIRC only like 3-4 out of every 10 lasers were actually usable.  2) The memory card thing. I mean, if you think about it... it's standard practice for a console. Why not get mad that the PS2 or Xbox didn't allow SD cards? Or the N64, or Game Cube... I get it, it's a dumb move. Sure. Do it for defeating piracy, I get that. Don't price it so stupidly high that your going to enrage customers though. THAT was the failure.  3) Yeah. The PSN hack. Yep. REALLLLLLLY stupid.
Sony's arrogant stance on cross-play... Also, PS Now being unreasonably priced for how lame it has been, especially at launch.  Though I think you nailed it with the top 3. Heck, 2 of those could well be in the top 3 mistakes from any big game company in the last decade.
Just one little comment on Sony Betamax. I think it‚Äôs not completely fair to compare it to Sony Memory Stick cards for the following reason. When Sony developed the Betamax tape format, there was not successful consumer home recording tape format on the market at the time.  Sony had tried to create such a market in 1972 with the release of the U-Matic videocassette format but that failed in the consumer for reasons such cost, the fact that issues with the tape magnetic coating formulations used early version of the U-Matic tape format often became unrealiable after on a short period of use, among other issues. Soon they pivot U-Matic towards the media production/TV studio market instead.  Phillips tried to compete with U-Magic in the early 70‚Äôs with their Video Cassette Recording (VCR) format (not to be confused with Video Cassette recorder (VCR‚Äôs). It also was not a success at the time. Other briefly existed too such as Cartrivision (built-in to select TV models and thus the first TV/VCR combos; also first home movie format), V-Cord (Sanyo), and VX (Aka The Great Time Machine) (Matsushita). None of those really took off in the consumer space outside of niche use by wealthy consumers. When Sony developed Betamax they were working with a market that did not yet exist. JVC was working on VHS at around the same time and Phillip‚Äôs followed their failed Video Cassette Recording format with Video2000, a much improved cassette format (over VCR). Video2000 only came out in Europe and never caught on, but for a while that meant that there were three competing formats in Europe. In the U.S. it was Betamax and VHS. The issue for Sony was not that they pushed their only proprietary format for home recording but rather mistakes made in the early design and how long they chose to remain in the market when it was clear that VHS was the winner of that format war.   The first mistake Sony make with Betamax was only having a maximum recording time of 60 min on Betamax I VCR‚Äôs, which was based on the fact that the earlier U-Matic format was only 60 min.  This was fine for U-Matic as it was by then primarily a TV studio/station format where most programs where an hour or less in length but unsuitable for home user who wanted to record a whole prime-time lineup of shows or a TV movie or sports broadcast. Another reason for the short Betamax I record time was the Sony being stuck on the smaller tape size vs VHS, thus limiting the amount of tape inside. VHS had larger tape cartridge size, thus more tape lengths and thus a 2 hour recording time out of the gate (later expanded that through the addition of EP/SLP recording modes that gave you up to 6 hours on a standard 120 cassette).  Betamax II fixed that issue but at the expense of BetaMax I‚Äôs slightly better picture quality vs VHS.    By the 1980‚Äôs Betamax and VHS were equal video quality-wise and largely competed only on features with each always being able to match the other in that area. Ultimately, the reason VHS won was likely a) Content producers/distributers and video rental stores favoring VHS, many different VCR manufacturers producing many VHS VCR models, and consumers buying VHS/VHS-C camcorders over the record-only failed Sony BetaMovie camcorders and thus wanting a VHS VCR for home movie playback. It was really only Sony refusing to see the writing on the wall until the late 80‚Äôs (when they produced their first VHS VCR) in this case.  This is quite different then the market Sony faced when they developed MemoryStick. There was already a successful market for memory card formats including SD/MicroSD, Smart Media, and Compact Flash. This is much like how Sony pushed it‚Äôs ATRAC audio codecs format over MP3 in it‚Äôs early MP3 players (ATRAC was derived from the codec used on MiniDiscs, much more successful product abate only in Japan and Europe).

This might sound like a nitpick, but generally in the computer space people use the word *storage* when they're talking about memory cards, not *memory* . The word memory is usually reserved for nonpermanent storage like RAM. Just something to consider for the future :)

It's an argument that's existed since the conception of the PS3, and those that know the PS3 will just never be able to properly convey it. I agree with EVERYTHING he mention, even as a huge fan of and advocate for Sony and PlayStation, which is impressive in and of itself as at the end of the day, I'm actually more of a 90's SEGA fan. SEGA dug their own grave, but it's Sony that burried them so you'd think I'd be bitter and I sort of was, but I'd been subjected to Sony long before as in my household my older brother and I shared a PlayStation while he had a Saturn and later got a Dreamcast. But, the second point he made regarding PS3, which for consumers and developers may be true as it caused a lot of heartache not only for consumers, but in a way, the industry suffered as a result of it, but I had no problem paying $500 for my 60GB PS3 and still feel it was worth it today. Why? Innovation. Just like Tim Sweeney stated the PS5 SSD is revolutionary and will change the way gaming is thought about, with a drive way ahead of PC architecture now, that's what the PS3 was. Sure it was said to be difficult to program for, I'm not a developer so I don't know, but look at what it's done for the industry. Xbox uses Blu-ray disc itself, not HD DVD. The Wii U used Blu-ray technology though Nintendo didn't use the Blu-ray name to save on license fees. Consoles and PCs now use 8-core processors, at the time it was only four.  Ken Kutaragi was an innovator, he was smart and ahead of his time that took so many others so much time to catch up, Kutaragi wanted to work, everyone else just wanted to get drunk. Today, everything is off the shelf because they saw what trying to be innovative did, cost a lot of money and frustrated lazy uneducated people and doesn't fit the universal business model. So, look at the PS5 and Xbox Series X. Identical hardware that leads to predictability or some corporate sabotage happened somewhere and now, technology advances at a much slower pace. Microsoft was using ATI while Sony was working with nVidia and consoles were totally different and unique, now, because everyone's afraid to offend and lose business, AMD owns the console market and nVidia are for the PC guys, so devs are eventually going to have to learn to get along with nVidia eventually when consoles fizzle out and the competition goes back to AMD vs Nvidia. But, now Microsoft can ask AMD well, what are you building for Sony? Oh, okay, just build that for us too and everyone will have the same hardware but can't play the competitions game. With the previous way of doing things, it was different hardware and thus a reason to buy both to see who could push their hardware the most, which all star development company Naughty Dog loves to do and Sony gives them the hardware allowing them to do what they do, wow the world, and the envy of Microsoft and Nintendo. The PS3 was revolutionary, at a cost it's true, but big leaps in technology aren't cheap, but the PS3 had a ten year life cycle, PS4 only had six.  Sony PS3 I'll argue. It's popular to say it was a failure but I disagree, the PS3 was a beast and really set the tone for the future of gaming while Microsoft just coasted along, and the PS3 still outsold the Xbox 360 overall, it had stamina and so to this day, Microsoft still always trails in third place, every generation.

I'm pretty sure the high cost PS3 being because of Bluray is factually wrong; it was because of the cell processor. The slow churning out of PS3s in it's early release was strictly because they couldn't get the processors fast enough.

11:50 "Sony was hacked again" Well, that's a common misconception. A DoS (or DDoS) is an attack, but it is not "hacking". It's analogous to telling everyone to call a customer service phone number repeatedly. It generates a lot of load, it blocks the service from real customers; but gives no further access. It's an attack, it's a nuisance, but it's not hacking.

Putting bluray amongst "Sony's top mistakes" in regards to the PS3 (under the jurisdiction of Sony Interactive Entertainment, the *division* or subsidiary *within* Sony responsible for the Playstation brand and PSN), despite Sony/SIE turning the PS3 ship around and eventually ending up with more lifetime sales than the 360, greater longevity, and the fact that Bluray has almost effectively replaced DVDs now (Sony won the new disc format war) is not only wrong, its silly. Moreover, Sony isn't just Playstation and the PSN (both of which, are again, under SIE). You're conflating two consoles and their networks to the two larger companies as a whole (of which the console business are a small part) and their overall internal infrastructure (different to the console's online networks) and business.
SONY is, IMHO, one of the worst companies out there in how they treat their customers.  Back around 2004/2005 I bought a Foo Fighters album (In Your Honor) on the day it came out. I really liked them and it was customary for the guys in our office to take turns listening to CDs through our PCs.  The next day I stuck the CD in my drive and I had to install software to play it.  That was weird, but I paid no mine.  I think there was bonus videos and such on it, so I just assumed that was part of it.  Nope! SONY had created a rootkit (that quickly became flagged as maleware by Norton) and it completely hosed my CD drive.  I couldn't use it.  This was a work PC and I think I had to format the dumb thing and just start over.  This "driver" was all in an effort to prevent piracy and SONY ended up with a sizable class action lawsuit against them because they felt they could modify (and maybe it was replace) device drivers on people machines just so they couldn't rip the disc.  I vowed after that to never, ever buy a new SONY product.  To the best of my knowledge, they've never gotten a single cent from me again and I hope they never do.

Some addendums to #3 and #2:  First, in the Vita's case, it really didn't help that even games that came physical still required an installation on the Vita memory card. So unlike the 3DS you had to have a large memory card since data were partially stored on the device, and it didn't help that quite a decent amount of (japanese) Vita games were released as digital-only releases in the West (often, the US would get a physical release whereas Europe would not as well).  On behalf of the PS3, the launch was even more of a disaster here in Europe. It was supposed to come out at the end of 2006, but due to a shortage in the production of the laser diodes for the blu-ray player caused the EU launch to be postponed for three months, resulting in Sony all but handing the EU holiday market to Nintendo on a silver platter.  On part of another "3 mistakes"... Atari sounds good, but MS has probably a few too.

#2 was a double whammy.  Sony spent a ton of money to win that format war believing they'd be able to make it back with royalties the same way the companies behind DVD continue to make money hand over fist on those royalties.  Unfortunately for them most consumers have either moved away from disc all together in favor of streaming or have stuck with the familiar DVD format (mostly because it's cheaper and the average consumer either doesn't care about the difference in quality, can't afford a newer setup, or simply doesn't know the difference).  I forget the exact numbers but estimates suggest Sony will never make up the money they lost on that bet.    As a side note I currently own 2 Blu-Rays and a Blue-Ray drive which should be able to play them (The Codecs needed to play Blu-Ray are freely available but some manufactures add some special proprietary features which can break compatibility unless you pay an extra $80 or so for a special Blu-Ray player program (in addition to the already expensive drive). Sony's positive (and negatives) is because they came from a giant conglomerate. Playstation was initially some weird skunkworks lead by "Ken" - and succeeded as they had the backing of Sony tech and money - but then became where the money was coming from as their other divisions imploded.  Ken became CEO of it all, and Sony's previous hardware lines were reduced to re-badging Korean hardware - they produce nice LG panels, if you prefer Android TV. In the midst of this turmoil (which has got to rank as one of the most disruptive transformations of a company that pulled through) it seemed that every division within Sony leapt in to ride the coat-tails to further their position, to mixed success. Some worked out like Blu-ray and UHD - others like every other proprietary memory format, just annoyed their users. Even those wins are a bit hollow, as we now can stream everything. (and have a legacy of annoyance in their users, forced to use these formats, and software).  Today if you have to think about what "Sony does best", it's quite a drop from their Wega screens and all the rest. Only think I can think of is their cameras - another skunkworks that excelled and is the name to have in your latest smartphone.   Maybe that's their strength? As others seem to boom and burst in the market they enter, Sony has always managed to spread their bets, and whatever happens remains in the game. Sega got pushed out, MS and Nintendo just buy tech from others, Sony whilst annoying, does still innovate.

I am surprised that you didn't mention the cell processors of the PS3.  It was weird and difficult for 3rd party developers to program for.  It has ripple effects of difficulty that to this day still leave legacy setups to the playstation line.  Emulation and backwards compatibility with PS3 games was not possible for the PS4 and likely won't be for PS5 because the architecture is so radically different.  Also, it made playstation 1 and 2 compatibility very difficult and expensive for the PS3.  Initially, they built a PS2 board inside of the PS3 for backwards compatibility at launch.  That made it bigger, hotter, and more expensive.  They eventually ripped it out to get the price down, but then you have most models of PS3 with no backwards compatibility.  Basically, almost everything about the PS3 was a mistake.  It did help them win for format war against HDDVD, but within just a few years that became irrelevant as streaming became more widespread and physical media is on the way out.  They did revitalize the PS3 in the second half of it's life, but it still fell slightly short of XBOX 360 in hardware sales and WAY BEHIND in software sales.

SONY‚Äôs refusal to allow Cross-Platform Play and Cross Platform Progression with PS4 and other platforms:   SONY received public scrutiny during E3 2018, with the release of Fortnite for Nintendo Switch, and denying PS4 users to carry-over their in-game progression to Switch. SONY argued that they were responsible to making sure PSN was a safe environment and by agreeing to their terms, you as the user were safe as a result. Everyone was irate at SONY with their decision, not only over Fortnite, but (Now Epic Games owned) Psyonix‚Äôs recently released version of Rocket League for Switch which also boasted Cross-play with PC and Xbox One. Both Microsoft and Nintendo even indirectly poked fun at SONY with the Bedrock update to the Switch version of Minecraft; by releasing an advertisement boasting Cross-platform play between Switch and Xbox One. While SONY did allow both cross-platform play and progression on PS4, the damage was already done.  Then there‚Äôs the arbitrary censorship they enforced on multiple titles including multiple visual novels, the Segran Kagura series, and even Dead or Alive Xtreme 3.  To which Nintendo president Shuntaro Furukawa simply argued that Nintendo‚Äôs stance on content is to confine ONLY to the ratings board, and anything more is unhealthy to the rest of the industry. Ironic considering the company‚Äôs [Nintendo] history with censorship on the NES and Super NES in America.

Edit: Also, I think the real mistake they made was the Cell CPU in the PS3. 3:33 People didn't came up with microSD to Vita adapters "soon". It took years and was a huge effort. Vita was "hacked" in late July 2016, over five years after release. Comparatively, this is ages, PSVita was pretty well secured. Most consoles are hacked within their relevancy period; just look at the Switch or PS3 or even PS4. After that, adapter schematics and code required to enable the adapter didn't show up until July of 2017. And then it took months for consumer-friendly adapters (like those shown in the video) to appear in online stores. People used to order custom PCBs off places like PCBWay and soldered microSD ports on their own.  By the way, I have 400 GB Vita, it's glorious. :)

Even though they call them memory cards, it's not accurate to refer to it as "memory." That's another way of saying RAM, which is volatile and doesn't persist when the system is shut off. A more accurate term is "storage."

DVDs didn't have the storage space necessary for the largest games at the time, and Bluray was superior to HD-DVD. It was a gamble that paid off as Bluray did win the optical disk wars.

I‚Äôll defend the PS3 including BluRay.  We have to remember that Sony makes a lot more than just video games. Sometimes they have to do things in the interest of other divisions of the company, like the thing with  PSP‚Äôs memory card (not the Vita thing, that was just greedy and stupid). Like the man said, those things were a competing format with the SD card, not some proprietary ‚Äúscrew you‚Äù to the consumers. The high launch price that came as a result of including BluRay on the PS3 may have been frustrating for gamers, but it was a gamble that paid off in the long term, for both Sony and consumers. It created an install base for BluRay that allowed the superior format to win, and win before too many people got invested in the loser. There was no decade-long period where people couldn‚Äôt always get the movies they wanted on the format they owned like there was with BetaMax and VHS a generation before, HD DVD died a quick and (mostly) painless death. Including BluRay also created an install base for the PS3, because a lot of people who weren‚Äôt strictly interested in games bought one anyway just because it was cheaper than a regular player. Many of them got into gaming because they already had the machine.

While we're on the subject of the Vita, I do not understand why so many people say the Vita was a good console.  Yes, it was really popular with homebrew, but only a small percentage of gamers are into homebrew.  Yes, it was a very powerful handheld for its time, but all that horsepower means nothing if your library of titles is practically non-existent.  In fact, all that power ended up being the Vita's downfall; on top of needing proprietary memory, it boasted that its specs were almost on par with then current home consoles.  The problem is that it made it too powerful for the majority of handheld developers to develop games for the system, as they just didn't have the budget to take full advantage of the hardware, but still not powerful enough to attract the big AAA developers who could afford to make games for the platform, but would stick to home consoles, both because they're more familiar with their architecture and devkits, and so they could squeeze out the extra horsepower those consoles provided.  When you take all of that into consideration, it's clear that the Vita was a failure on almost every fronts, and is a testament to how little Sony understood the market of the time.

Another reason why the early PS3s were expensive was the Western and JP versions had an extra CPU, the Emotion Engine, which was a proprietary CPU for the PS2. They would remove this in future PS3s with software backwards compatibility. However, those who bought the software-based compatibility versions would soon find out that there were some games that didn't work with the software-based backwards compatibility. The Director's Cut of Tales of Destiny had a anti-piracy in place that disallowed the game to be run on emulators. A certain chest that had a key item required to continue the game had a tile in front of the chest that would have no collision if the game was run on an emulator. This includes the software-based backwards compatible PS3s.

Sony's Big Mistakes: 1. Focusing on Graphics When Affordability and Casual-Friendliness Are Paramount in Handheld Gaming with the PlayStation Portable. 2. Sony's console engineers getting way too high off their egos and designing a convoluted and very expensive architecture for the PlayStation 3, making it 2nd and 3rd party developer unfriendly, and adding a blu-ray drive that made the system way too expensive ($599 launch price, ouch). 3. Abandoning and shuttering most of their second-party studios during the great studio die-off of the PS3 generation (from 2007 to 2011). Zipper Interactive (makers of SOCOM), Incognito Entertainment (Twisted Metal & Warhawk/Starhawk), Superbot Games (PlayStation All-Stars), LightBox Interactive (successor to Igcognito), Psygnosis/Sony Liverpool (Lemmings, Wipeout, Colony Wars, and G-Police), Sony Cambridge (Medievil 1 & 2), and even Sony's own internal development teams responsible for the Jumping Flash and Ape Escape games.
